<p><b>Jazz</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Music_genre" title="Music genre">music genre</a> that originated in the <a href="/wiki/African_Americans" title="African Americans">African-American</a> communities of <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a> in the late 19th and early 20th centuries, with its roots in <a href="/wiki/Blues" title="Blues">blues</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ragtime" title="Ragtime">ragtime</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-4">[4]</a></sup> Since the 1920s <a href="/wiki/Jazz_Age" title="Jazz Age">Jazz Age</a>, it has been recognized as a major form of musical expression in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_music" title="Traditional music">traditional</a> and <a href="/wiki/Popular_music" title="Popular music">popular music</a>. Jazz is characterized by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swung_note" title="Swung note">swing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blue_note" title="Blue note">blue notes</a>, complex <a href="/wiki/Chord_(music)" title="Chord (music)">chords</a>, <a href="/wiki/Call_and_response_(music)" title="Call and response (music)">call and response vocals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polyrhythm" title="Polyrhythm">polyrhythms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jazz_improvisation" title="Jazz improvisation">improvisation</a>. Jazz has roots in European <a href="/wiki/Harmony" title="Harmony">harmony</a> and African rhythmic rituals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>As jazz spread around the world, it drew on national, regional, and local musical cultures, which gave rise to different styles. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dixieland" title="Dixieland">New Orleans jazz</a> began in the early 1910s, combining earlier <a href="/wiki/Brass_band" title="Brass band">brass band</a> marches, French <a href="/wiki/Quadrille" title="Quadrille">quadrilles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biguine" title="Biguine">biguine</a>, ragtime and blues with collective <a href="/wiki/Polyphony" title="Polyphony">polyphonic</a> <a href="/wiki/Musical_improvisation" title="Musical improvisation">improvisation</a>. But jazz did not begin as a single musical tradition in New Orleans or elsewhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hennessey_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hennessey-7">[7]</a></sup> In the 1930s, arranged dance-oriented <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swing_(music)" title="Swing (music)">swing</a> <a href="/wiki/Big_band" title="Big band">big bands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kansas_City_jazz" title="Kansas City jazz">Kansas City jazz</a> (a hard-swinging, bluesy, improvisational style), and <a href="/wiki/Gypsy_jazz" title="Gypsy jazz">gypsy jazz</a> (a style that emphasized <a href="/wiki/Bal-musette" title="Bal-musette">musette</a> waltzes) were the prominent styles. <a href="/wiki/Bebop" title="Bebop">Bebop</a> emerged in the 1940s, shifting jazz from danceable popular music toward a more challenging "musician's music" which was played at faster tempos and used more chord-based improvisation. <a href="/wiki/Cool_jazz" title="Cool jazz">Cool jazz</a> developed near the end of the 1940s, introducing calmer, smoother sounds and long, linear melodic lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>The mid-1950s saw the emergence of <a href="/wiki/Hard_bop" title="Hard bop">hard bop</a>, which introduced influences from <a href="/wiki/Rhythm_and_blues" title="Rhythm and blues">rhythm and blues</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gospel_music" title="Gospel music">gospel</a>, and blues to small groups and particularly to saxophone and piano. <a href="/wiki/Modal_jazz" title="Modal jazz">Modal jazz</a> developed in the late 1950s, using the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musical_mode" title="Musical mode">mode</a>, or musical scale, as the basis of musical structure and improvisation, as did <a href="/wiki/Free_jazz" title="Free jazz">free jazz</a>, which explored playing without regular meter, beat and formal structures. <a href="/wiki/Jazz_fusion" title="Jazz fusion">Jazz-rock fusion</a> appeared in the late 1960s and early 1970s, combining jazz improvisation with <a href="/wiki/Rock_music" title="Rock music">rock music</a>'s rhythms, electric instruments, and highly amplified stage sound. In the early 1980s, a commercial form of jazz fusion called <a href="/wiki/Smooth_jazz" title="Smooth jazz">smooth jazz</a> became successful, garnering significant radio airplay. Other styles and genres abound in the 2000s, such as <a href="/wiki/Latin_jazz" title="Latin jazz">Latin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Afro-Cuban_jazz" title="Afro-Cuban jazz">Afro-Cuban jazz</a>.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_definition"><span id="Definition"></span> Etymology and definition</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jazz_(word)" title="Jazz (word)"><i>Jazz</i> (word)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EubieBlake.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="244" data-file-width="200" decoding="async" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/eb/EubieBlake.jpg" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EubieBlake.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>American jazz composer, lyricist, and pianist <a href="/wiki/Eubie_Blake" title="Eubie Blake">Eubie Blake</a> made an early contribution to the genre's etymology</div></div></div>
<p>The origin of the word <i><a href="/wiki/Jazz_(word)" title="Jazz (word)">jazz</a></i> has resulted in considerable research, and its history is well documented. It is believed to be related to <i>jasm</i>, a slang term dating back to 1860 meaning "pep, energy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baseball_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-baseball-9">[9]</a></sup> The earliest written record of the word is in a 1912 article in the <i><a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Times" title="Los Angeles Times">Los Angeles Times</a></i> in which a minor league baseball pitcher described a pitch which he called a "jazz ball" "because it wobbles and you simply can't do anything with it".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baseball_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-baseball-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>The use of the word in a musical context was documented as early as 1915 in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chicago_Daily_Tribune" title="Chicago Daily Tribune">Chicago Daily Tribune</a>.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDT_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDT-10">[10]</a></sup> Its first documented use in a musical context in New Orleans was in a November 14, 1916, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Times-Picayune" title="Times-Picayune">Times-Picayune</a></i> article about "jas bands".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> In an interview with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Public_Radio" title="National Public Radio">National Public Radio</a>, musician <a href="/wiki/Eubie_Blake" title="Eubie Blake">Eubie Blake</a> offered his recollections of the slang connotations of the term, saying: "When Broadway picked it up, they called it 'J-A-Z-Z'. It wasn't called that. It was spelled 'J-A-S-S'. That was dirty, and if you knew what it was, you wouldn't say it in front of ladies."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vitale_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vitale-12">[12]</a></sup> The American Dialect Society named it the <a href="/wiki/Word_of_the_year" title="Word of the year">Word of the 20th Century</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-century_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-century-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Albert_Gleizes,_1915,_Composition_pour_Jazz,_oil_on_cardboard,_73_x_73_cm,_Solomon_R._Guggenheim_Museum,_New_York.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3999" data-file-width="3993" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/03/Albert_Gleizes%2C_1915%2C_Composition_pour_Jazz%2C_oil_on_cardboard%2C_73_x_73_cm%2C_Solomon_R._Guggenheim_Museum%2C_New_York.jpg/220px-Albert_Gleizes%2C_1915%2C_Composition_pour_Jazz%2C_oil_on_cardboard%2C_73_x_73_cm%2C_Solomon_R._Guggenheim_Museum%2C_New_York.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/03/Albert_Gleizes%2C_1915%2C_Composition_pour_Jazz%2C_oil_on_cardboard%2C_73_x_73_cm%2C_Solomon_R._Guggenheim_Museum%2C_New_York.jpg/330px-Albert_Gleizes%2C_1915%2C_Composition_pour_Jazz%2C_oil_on_cardboard%2C_73_x_73_cm%2C_Solomon_R._Guggenheim_Museum%2C_New_York.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/03/Albert_Gleizes%2C_1915%2C_Composition_pour_Jazz%2C_oil_on_cardboard%2C_73_x_73_cm%2C_Solomon_R._Guggenheim_Museum%2C_New_York.jpg/440px-Albert_Gleizes%2C_1915%2C_Composition_pour_Jazz%2C_oil_on_cardboard%2C_73_x_73_cm%2C_Solomon_R._Guggenheim_Museum%2C_New_York.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Albert_Gleizes,_1915,_Composition_pour_Jazz,_oil_on_cardboard,_73_x_73_cm,_Solomon_R._Guggenheim_Museum,_New_York.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Albert_Gleizes" title="Albert Gleizes">Albert Gleizes</a>, 1915, <i><a href="/wiki/Composition_for_%22Jazz%22" title='Composition for "Jazz"'>Composition for "Jazz"</a></i> from the Solomon R. Guggenheim Museum, New York</div></div></div>
<p>Jazz is difficult to define because it encompasses a wide range of music spanning a period of over 100 years, from <a href="/wiki/Ragtime" title="Ragtime">ragtime</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Rock_music" title="Rock music">rock</a>-infused <a href="/wiki/Jazz_fusion" title="Jazz fusion">fusion</a>. Attempts have been made to define jazz from the perspective of other musical traditions, such as European music history or African music. But critic <a href="/wiki/Joachim-Ernst_Berendt" title="Joachim-Ernst Berendt">Joachim-Ernst Berendt</a> argues that its terms of reference and its definition should be broader,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Joachim_E._Berendt_1981._Page_371_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Joachim_E._Berendt_1981._Page_371-14">[14]</a></sup> defining jazz as a "form of <a href="/wiki/Art_music" title="Art music">art music</a> which originated in the United States through the confrontation of the Negro with European music"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berendt1964_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berendt1964-15">[15]</a></sup> and arguing that it differs from European music in that jazz has a "special relationship to time defined as 'swing<span style="padding-right:.15em;">'</span>". Jazz involves "a spontaneity and vitality of musical production in which improvisation plays a role" and contains a "sonority and manner of phrasing which mirror the individuality of the performing jazz musician".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Joachim_E._Berendt_1981._Page_371_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Joachim_E._Berendt_1981._Page_371-14">[14]</a></sup> In the opinion of <a href="/wiki/Robert_Christgau" title="Robert Christgau">Robert Christgau</a>, "most of us would say that inventing meaning while letting loose is the essence and promise of jazz".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>A broader definition that encompasses different eras of jazz has been proposed by Travis Jackson: "it is music that includes qualities such as swing, improvising, group interaction, developing an 'individual voice', and being open to different musical possibilities".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elsdon_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elsdon-17">[17]</a></sup> Krin Gibbard argued that "jazz is a construct" which designates "a number of musics with enough in common to be understood as part of a coherent tradition".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> In contrast to commentators who have argued for excluding types of jazz, musicians are sometimes reluctant to define the music they play. <a href="/wiki/Duke_Ellington" title="Duke Ellington">Duke Ellington</a>, one of jazz's most famous figures, said, "It's all music."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Elements">Elements</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Improvisation">Improvisation</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jazz_improvisation" title="Jazz improvisation">Jazz improvisation</a></div>
<p>Although jazz is considered difficult to define, in part because it contains many subgenres, <a href="/wiki/Musical_improvisation" title="Musical improvisation">improvisation</a> is one of its defining elements. The centrality of improvisation is attributed to the influence of earlier forms of music such as <a href="/wiki/Blues" title="Blues">blues</a>, a form of folk music which arose in part from the <a href="/wiki/Work_song" title="Work song">work songs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Field_holler" title="Field holler">field hollers</a> of African-American slaves on plantations. These work songs were commonly structured around a repetitive <a href="/wiki/Call_and_response_(music)" title="Call and response (music)">call-and-response</a> pattern, but early blues was also improvisational. <a href="/wiki/Classical_music" title="Classical music">Classical music</a> performance is evaluated more by its fidelity to the <a href="/wiki/Sheet_music" title="Sheet music">musical score</a>, with less attention given to interpretation, ornamentation, and accompaniment. The classical performer's goal is to play the composition as it was written. In contrast, jazz is often characterized by the product of interaction and collaboration, placing less value on the contribution of the composer, if there is one, and more on the performer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiddins199870_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiddins199870-20">[20]</a></sup> The jazz performer interprets a tune in individual ways, never playing the same composition twice. Depending on the performer's mood, experience, and interaction with band members or audience members, the performer may change melodies, harmonies, and time signatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiddins199889_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiddins199889-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>In early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dixieland" title="Dixieland">Dixieland</a>, a.k.a. New Orleans jazz, performers took turns playing melodies and improvising <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Countermelodies" title="Countermelodies">countermelodies</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Swing_music" title="Swing music">swing</a> era of the 1920s–'40s, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Big_bands" title="Big bands">big bands</a> relied more on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arrangements" title="Arrangements">arrangements</a> which were written or learned by ear and memorized. Soloists improvised within these arrangements. In the <a href="/wiki/Bebop" title="Bebop">bebop</a> era of the 1940s, big bands gave way to small groups and minimal arrangements in which the melody was stated briefly at the beginning and most of the piece was improvised. <a href="/wiki/Modal_jazz" title="Modal jazz">Modal jazz</a> abandoned <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chord_progressions" title="Chord progressions">chord progressions</a> to allow musicians to improvise even more. In many forms of jazz, a soloist is supported by a <a href="/wiki/Rhythm_section" title="Rhythm section">rhythm section</a> of one or more chordal instruments (piano, guitar), double bass, and drums. The rhythm section plays chords and rhythms that outline the composition structure and complement the soloist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Avant-garde_jazz" title="Avant-garde jazz">avant-garde</a> and <a href="/wiki/Free_jazz" title="Free jazz">free jazz</a>, the separation of soloist and band is reduced, and there is license, or even a requirement, for the abandoning of chords, scales, and meters.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditionalism">Traditionalism</span></h3>
<p>Since the emergence of bebop, forms of jazz that are commercially oriented or influenced by popular music have been criticized. According to Bruce Johnson, there has always been a "tension between jazz as a commercial music and an art form".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elsdon_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Elsdon-17">[17]</a></sup> Regarding the <a href="/wiki/Dixieland_jazz" title="Dixieland jazz">Dixieland jazz</a> revival of the 1940s, black musicians rejected it as being shallow nostalgia entertainment for white audiences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baraka_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baraka-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davis-24">[24]</a></sup> On the other hand, traditional jazz enthusiasts have dismissed bebop, free jazz, and jazz fusion as forms of debasement and betrayal. An alternative view is that jazz can absorb and transform diverse musical styles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gilbert_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-gilbert-25">[25]</a></sup> By avoiding the creation of norms, jazz allows avant-garde styles to emerge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elsdon_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Elsdon-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diversity_in_jazz">Diversity in jazz</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jazz_and_race">Jazz and race</span></h3>
<p>For some African Americans, jazz has drawn attention to African-American contributions to culture and history. For others, jazz is a reminder of "an oppressive and racist society and restrictions on their artistic visions".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Amiri_Baraka" title="Amiri Baraka">Amiri Baraka</a> argues that there is a "white jazz" genre that expresses <a href="/wiki/Whiteness_studies" title="Whiteness studies">whiteness</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BarakaReader_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-BarakaReader-27">[27]</a></sup> White jazz musicians appeared in the midwest and in other areas throughout the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Papa_Jack_Laine" title="Papa Jack Laine">Papa Jack Laine</a>, who ran the Reliance band in New Orleans in the 1910s, was called "the father of white jazz".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yurochko_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yurochko-28">[28]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Original_Dixieland_Jazz_Band" title="Original Dixieland Jazz Band">Original Dixieland Jazz Band</a>, whose members were white, were the first jazz group to record, and <a href="/wiki/Bix_Beiderbecke" title="Bix Beiderbecke">Bix Beiderbecke</a> was one of the most prominent jazz soloists of the 1920s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Larkin_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Larkin-29">[29]</a></sup> The Chicago Style was developed by white musicians such as <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Condon" title="Eddie Condon">Eddie Condon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bud_Freeman" title="Bud Freeman">Bud Freeman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_McPartland" title="Jimmy McPartland">Jimmy McPartland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dave_Tough" title="Dave Tough">Dave Tough</a>. Others from Chicago such as <a href="/wiki/Benny_Goodman" title="Benny Goodman">Benny Goodman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gene_Krupa" title="Gene Krupa">Gene Krupa</a> became leading members of swing during the 1930s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-midwest_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-midwest-30">[30]</a></sup> Many bands included both black and white musicians. These musicians helped change attitudes toward race in the U.S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HowHentoff_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-HowHentoff-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roles_of_women">Roles of women</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ethel_Waters_-_William_P._Gottlieb.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5149" data-file-width="4915" decoding="async" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Ethel_Waters_-_William_P._Gottlieb.jpg/175px-Ethel_Waters_-_William_P._Gottlieb.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Ethel_Waters_-_William_P._Gottlieb.jpg/263px-Ethel_Waters_-_William_P._Gottlieb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Ethel_Waters_-_William_P._Gottlieb.jpg/350px-Ethel_Waters_-_William_P._Gottlieb.jpg 2x" width="175"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ethel_Waters_-_William_P._Gottlieb.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ethel_Waters" title="Ethel Waters">Ethel Waters</a> sang "<a href="/wiki/Stormy_Weather_(song)" title="Stormy Weather (song)">Stormy Weather</a>" at the <a href="/wiki/Cotton_Club" title="Cotton Club">Cotton Club</a>.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Women_in_jazz" title="Women in jazz">Women in jazz</a></div>
<p>Female jazz performers and composers have contributed to jazz throughout its history. Although <a href="/wiki/Betty_Carter" title="Betty Carter">Betty Carter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ella_Fitzgerald" title="Ella Fitzgerald">Ella Fitzgerald</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adelaide_Hall" title="Adelaide Hall">Adelaide Hall</a>, <a href="/wiki/Billie_Holiday" title="Billie Holiday">Billie Holiday</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abbey_Lincoln" title="Abbey Lincoln">Abbey Lincoln</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anita_O%27Day" title="Anita O'Day">Anita O'Day</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dinah_Washington" title="Dinah Washington">Dinah Washington</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ethel_Waters" title="Ethel Waters">Ethel Waters</a> were recognized for their vocal talent, less familiar were bandleaders, composers, and instrumentalists such as pianist <a href="/wiki/Lil_Hardin_Armstrong" title="Lil Hardin Armstrong">Lil Hardin Armstrong</a>, trumpeter <a href="/wiki/Valaida_Snow" title="Valaida Snow">Valaida Snow</a>, and songwriters <a href="/wiki/Irene_Higginbotham" title="Irene Higginbotham">Irene Higginbotham</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dorothy_Fields" title="Dorothy Fields">Dorothy Fields</a>. Women began playing instruments in jazz in the early 1920s, drawing particular recognition on piano.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murph_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Murph-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>When male jazz musicians were drafted during World War II, many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/All-female_bands" title="All-female bands">all-female bands</a> replaced them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murph_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Murph-32">[32]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_International_Sweethearts_of_Rhythm" title="The International Sweethearts of Rhythm">The International Sweethearts of Rhythm</a>, which was founded in 1937, was a popular band that became the first all-female integrated band in the U.S. and the first to travel with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USO" title="USO">USO</a>, touring Europe in 1945. Women were members of the big bands of <a href="/wiki/Woody_Herman" title="Woody Herman">Woody Herman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Wilson" title="Gerald Wilson">Gerald Wilson</a>. Beginning in the 1950s, many women jazz instrumentalists were prominent, some sustaining long careers. Some of the most distinctive improvisers, composers, and bandleaders in jazz have been women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Placksin_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Placksin-33">[33]</a></sup> Trombonist <a href="/wiki/Melba_Liston" title="Melba Liston">Melba Liston</a> is acknowledged as the first female horn player to work in major bands and to make a real impact on jazz, not only as a musician but also as a respected composer and arranger, particularly through her collaborations with <a href="/wiki/Randy_Weston" title="Randy Weston">Randy Weston</a> from the late 1950s into the 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jews_in_jazz">Jews in jazz</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jews_in_jazz" title="Jews in jazz">Jews in jazz</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Al_Jolson_-_publicity.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="833" data-file-width="667" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Al_Jolson_-_publicity.JPG/175px-Al_Jolson_-_publicity.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Al_Jolson_-_publicity.JPG/263px-Al_Jolson_-_publicity.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Al_Jolson_-_publicity.JPG/350px-Al_Jolson_-_publicity.JPG 2x" width="175"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Al_Jolson_-_publicity.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Al_Jolson" title="Al Jolson">Al Jolson</a> in 1929</div></div></div>
<p>Jewish Americans played a significant role in jazz. As jazz spread, it developed to encompass many different cultures, and the work of Jewish composers in <a href="/wiki/Tin_Pan_Alley" title="Tin Pan Alley">Tin Pan Alley</a> helped shape the many different sounds that jazz came to incorporate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jewish Americans were able to thrive in Jazz because of the probationary whiteness that they were allotted at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> George Bornstein wrote that African Americans were sympathetic to the plight of the Jewish American and vice versa. As disenfranchised minorities themselves, Jewish composers of popular music saw themselves as natural allies with African Americans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/The_Jazz_Singer" title="The Jazz Singer">The Jazz Singer</a></i> with <a href="/wiki/Al_Jolson" title="Al Jolson">Al Jolson</a> is one example of how Jewish Americans were able to bring jazz, music that African Americans developed, into popular culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Benny_Goodman" title="Benny Goodman">Benny Goodman</a> was a vital Jewish American to the progression of Jazz. Goodman was the leader of a racially integrated band named King of Swing. His jazz concert in the <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Hall" title="Carnegie Hall">Carnegie Hall</a> in 1938 was the first ever to be played there. The concert was described by Bruce Eder as "the single most important jazz or popular music concert in history".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_and_early_history">Origins and early history</span></h2>
<p>Jazz originated in the late-19th to early-20th century.  It developed out of many forms of music, including <a href="/wiki/Blues" title="Blues">blues</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spirituals" title="Spirituals">spirituals</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hymns" title="Hymns">hymns</a>, <a href="/wiki/March_(music)" title="March (music)">marches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vaudeville" title="Vaudeville">vaudeville</a> song, <a href="/wiki/Ragtime" title="Ragtime">ragtime</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dance_music" title="Dance music">dance music</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encyclopedia_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-encyclopedia-41">[41]</a></sup> It also incorporated interpretations of American and European classical music, entwined with African and slave folk songs and the influences of West African culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-listverse_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-listverse-42">[42]</a></sup> Its composition and style have changed many times throughout the years with each performer's personal interpretation and improvisation, which is also one of the greatest appeals of the genre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-definejazz_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-definejazz-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blended_African_and_European_music_sensibilities">Blended African and European music sensibilities</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dancing_in_Congo_Square_-_Edward_Winsor_Kemble,_1886.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="412" data-file-width="837" decoding="async" height="108" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Dancing_in_Congo_Square_-_Edward_Winsor_Kemble%2C_1886.jpg/220px-Dancing_in_Congo_Square_-_Edward_Winsor_Kemble%2C_1886.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Dancing_in_Congo_Square_-_Edward_Winsor_Kemble%2C_1886.jpg/330px-Dancing_in_Congo_Square_-_Edward_Winsor_Kemble%2C_1886.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Dancing_in_Congo_Square_-_Edward_Winsor_Kemble%2C_1886.jpg/440px-Dancing_in_Congo_Square_-_Edward_Winsor_Kemble%2C_1886.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dancing_in_Congo_Square_-_Edward_Winsor_Kemble,_1886.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dance in Congo Square in the late 1700s, artist's conception by <a href="/wiki/E._W._Kemble" title="E. W. Kemble">E. W. Kemble</a> from a century later</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Slave_dance_to_banjo,_1780s.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="651" data-file-width="982" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Slave_dance_to_banjo%2C_1780s.jpg/220px-Slave_dance_to_banjo%2C_1780s.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Slave_dance_to_banjo%2C_1780s.jpg/330px-Slave_dance_to_banjo%2C_1780s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Slave_dance_to_banjo%2C_1780s.jpg/440px-Slave_dance_to_banjo%2C_1780s.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Slave_dance_to_banjo,_1780s.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In the late 18th-century painting <i><a href="/wiki/The_Old_Plantation" title="The Old Plantation">The Old Plantation</a></i>, African-Americans dance to banjo and percussion.</div></div></div>
<p>By the 18th century, slaves in the New Orleans area gathered socially at a special market, in an area which later became known as Congo Square, famous for its African dances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1866, the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">Atlantic slave trade</a> had brought nearly 400,000 Africans to North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> The slaves came largely from <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a> and the greater <a href="/wiki/Congo_River" title="Congo River">Congo River</a> basin and brought strong musical traditions with them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke19997–9_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke19997–9-46">[46]</a></sup> The African traditions primarily use a single-line melody and <a href="/wiki/Call_and_response_(music)" title="Call and response (music)">call-and-response</a> pattern, and the rhythms have a <a href="/wiki/Cross-beat" title="Cross-beat">counter-metric</a> structure and reflect African speech patterns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>An 1885 account says that they were making strange music (Creole) on an equally strange variety of 'instruments'—washboards, washtubs, jugs, boxes beaten with sticks or bones and a drum made by stretching skin over a flour-barrel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hearn2017_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hearn2017-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Lavish festivals with African-based dances to drums were organized on Sundays at Place Congo, or <a href="/wiki/Congo_Square" title="Congo Square">Congo Square</a>, in New Orleans until 1843.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> There are historical accounts of other music and dance gatherings elsewhere in the southern United States. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Palmer_(writer)" title="Robert Palmer (writer)">Robert Palmer</a> said of percussive slave music:
</p>
<blockquote><p>Usually such music was associated with annual festivals, when the year's crop was harvested and several days were set aside for celebration. As late as 1861, a traveler in North Carolina saw dancers dressed in costumes that included horned headdresses and cow tails and heard music provided by a sheepskin-covered "gumbo box", apparently a frame drum; triangles and jawbones furnished the auxiliary percussion. There are quite a few [accounts] from the southeastern states and Louisiana dating from the period 1820–1850. Some of the earliest [Mississippi] Delta settlers came from the vicinity of New Orleans, where drumming was never actively discouraged for very long and homemade drums were used to accompany public dancing until the outbreak of the Civil War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PalmerDeep_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-PalmerDeep-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>Another influence came from the harmonic style of <a href="/wiki/Hymn" title="Hymn">hymns</a> of the church, which black slaves had learned and incorporated into their own music as <a href="/wiki/Spirituals" title="Spirituals">spirituals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke199914–17,_27–28_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke199914–17,_27–28-51">[51]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Origins_of_the_blues" title="Origins of the blues">origins of the blues</a> are undocumented, though they can be seen as the secular counterpart of the spirituals. However, as <a href="/wiki/Gerhard_Kubik" title="Gerhard Kubik">Gerhard Kubik</a> points out, whereas the spirituals are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homophonic" title="Homophonic">homophonic</a>, rural blues and early jazz "was largely based on concepts of <a href="/wiki/Heterophony" title="Heterophony">heterophony</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKubik1999112_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKubik1999112-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Virginia_Minstrels,_1843.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="817" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Virginia_Minstrels%2C_1843.jpg/220px-Virginia_Minstrels%2C_1843.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Virginia_Minstrels%2C_1843.jpg/330px-Virginia_Minstrels%2C_1843.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Virginia_Minstrels%2C_1843.jpg/440px-Virginia_Minstrels%2C_1843.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Virginia_Minstrels,_1843.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The blackface <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Minstrels" title="Virginia Minstrels">Virginia Minstrels</a> in 1843, featuring tambourine, fiddle, banjo and <a href="/wiki/Bones_(instrument)" title="Bones (instrument)">bones</a></div></div></div>
<p>During the early 19th century an increasing number of black musicians learned to play European instruments, particularly the violin, which they used to parody European dance music in their own <a href="/wiki/Cakewalk" title="Cakewalk">cakewalk</a> dances. In turn, European-American <a href="/wiki/Minstrel_show" title="Minstrel show">minstrel show</a> performers in <a href="/wiki/Blackface" title="Blackface">blackface</a> popularized the music internationally, combining <a href="/wiki/Syncopation" title="Syncopation">syncopation</a> with European harmonic accompaniment. In the mid-1800s the white New Orleans composer <a href="/wiki/Louis_Moreau_Gottschalk" title="Louis Moreau Gottschalk">Louis Moreau Gottschalk</a> adapted slave rhythms and melodies from Cuba and other Caribbean islands into piano salon music. New Orleans was the main nexus between the Afro-Caribbean and African-American cultures.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="African_rhythmic_retention">African rhythmic retention</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Traditional_sub-Saharan_African_harmony" title="Traditional sub-Saharan African harmony">Traditional sub-Saharan African harmony</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Black_Codes_(United_States)" title="Black Codes (United States)">Black Codes</a> outlawed drumming by slaves, which meant that African drumming traditions were not preserved in North America, unlike in Cuba, Haiti, and elsewhere in the Caribbean. African-based rhythmic patterns were retained in the United States in large part through "body rhythms" such as stomping, clapping, and <a href="/wiki/Juba_dance" title="Juba dance">patting juba dancing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palmer39_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-palmer39-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the opinion of jazz historian <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Borneman" title="Ernest Borneman">Ernest Borneman</a>, what preceded New Orleans jazz before 1890 was "Afro-Latin music", similar to what was played in the Caribbean at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> A three-stroke pattern known in Cuban music as <a href="/wiki/Tresillo_(rhythm)" title="Tresillo (rhythm)"><i>tresillo</i></a> is a fundamental rhythmic figure heard in many different slave musics of the Caribbean, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Afro-Caribbean_music" title="Afro-Caribbean music">Afro-Caribbean</a> folk dances performed in New Orleans <a href="/wiki/Congo_Square" title="Congo Square">Congo Square</a> and Gottschalk's compositions (for example "Souvenirs From Havana" (1859)). Tresillo (shown below) is the most basic and most prevalent duple-pulse rhythmic <a href="/wiki/Cell_(music)" title="Cell (music)">cell</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_African_music_traditions" title="Sub-Saharan African music traditions">sub-Saharan African music traditions</a> and the music of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_Diaspora" title="African Diaspora">African Diaspora</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sublette_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sublette-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeñalosa201038–46_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeñalosa201038–46-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><div class="mw-ext-score" data-midi="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/c/l/clpo3cchxlz6u5xxgbibd8bi5y6md0u/clpo3cch.midi"><img alt="
\new RhythmicStaff {
   \clef percussion
   \time 2/4
   \repeat volta 2 { c8. c16 r8[ c] }
}
" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/c/l/clpo3cchxlz6u5xxgbibd8bi5y6md0u/clpo3cch.png" width="144"/><audio controls="" data-durationhint="3" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Tresillo_divisive.mid" id="mwe_player_0" preload="none" style="width:220px;" width="220"><source data-bandwidth="0" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/8/8c/Tresillo_divisive.mid/Tresillo_divisive.mid.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/><source data-bandwidth="0" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg Vorbis" data-title="Ogg Vorbis" data-transcodekey="ogg" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/8/8c/Tresillo_divisive.mid/Tresillo_divisive.mid.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="707" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="⧼timedmedia-midi⧽ source" data-title="Original ⧼timedmedia-midi⧽ file (707 bps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8c/Tresillo_divisive.mid" type="audio/midi"/></audio></div></dd></dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Tresillo_(rhythm)" title="Tresillo (rhythm)">Tresillo</a> is heard prominently in New Orleans <a href="/wiki/Second_line_(parades)" title="Second line (parades)">second line</a> music and in other forms of popular music from that city from the turn of the 20th century to present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> "By and large the simpler African rhythmic patterns survived in jazz ... because they could be adapted more readily to European rhythmic conceptions," jazz historian <a href="/wiki/Gunther_Schuller" title="Gunther Schuller">Gunther Schuller</a> observed. "Some survived, others were discarded as the Europeanization progressed."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchuller196819_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchuller196819-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the post-Civil War period (after 1865), African Americans were able to obtain surplus military bass drums, snare drums and fifes, and an original African-American drum and fife music emerged, featuring tresillo and related syncopated rhythmic figures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKubik199952_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKubik199952-59">[59]</a></sup> This was a drumming tradition that was distinct from its Caribbean counterparts, expressing a uniquely African-American sensibility. "The snare and bass drummers played syncopated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cross_beat" title="Cross beat">cross-rhythms</a>," observed the writer Robert Palmer, speculating that "this tradition must have dated back to the latter half of the nineteenth century, and it could have not have developed in the first place if there hadn't been a reservoir of polyrhythmic sophistication in the culture it nurtured."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palmer39_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-palmer39-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Afro-Cuban_influence">Afro-Cuban influence</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_African_heritage_in_Cuba" title="Music of African heritage in Cuba">Music of African heritage in Cuba</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/African-American_music" title="African-American music">African-American music</a> began incorporating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afro-Cuban" title="Afro-Cuban">Afro-Cuban</a> rhythmic motifs in the 19th century when the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habanera_(music)" title="Habanera (music)">habanera</a> (Cuban <a href="/wiki/Contradanza" title="Contradanza">contradanza</a>) gained international popularity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Musicians from <a href="/wiki/Havana" title="Havana">Havana</a> and New Orleans would take the twice-daily ferry between both cities to perform, and the habanera quickly took root in the musically fertile Crescent City. <a href="/wiki/John_Storm_Roberts" title="John Storm Roberts">John Storm Roberts</a> states that the musical genre habanera "reached the U.S. twenty years before the first rag was published."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JSRoberts_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-JSRoberts-61">[61]</a></sup> For the more than quarter-century in which the <a href="/wiki/Cakewalk" title="Cakewalk">cakewalk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ragtime" title="Ragtime">ragtime</a>, and proto-jazz were forming and developing, the habanera was a consistent part of African-American popular music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JSRoberts_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-JSRoberts-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Habaneras were widely available as sheet music and were the first written music which was rhythmically based on an African motif (1803).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manuel_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Manuel-62">[62]</a></sup> From the perspective of African-American music, the "habanera rhythm" (also known as "congo"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manuel_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Manuel-62">[62]</a></sup> "tango-congo",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Acosta_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Acosta-63">[63]</a></sup> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tango_(music)" title="Tango (music)">tango</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salsa_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Salsa-64">[64]</a></sup> can be thought of as a combination of <a href="/wiki/Tresillo_(rhythm)" title="Tresillo (rhythm)">tresillo</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Beat_(music)" title="Beat (music)">backbeat</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeñalosa201042_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeñalosa201042-65">[65]</a></sup> The habanera was the first of many Cuban music genres which enjoyed periods of popularity in the United States and reinforced and inspired the use of tresillo-based rhythms in African-American music.
</p>
<dl><dd><div class="mw-ext-score" data-midi="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/c/r/cre08sn2d1zsf4q0adfpv6hmsfskbne/cre08sn2.midi"><img alt="
    \new Staff &lt;&lt;
       \relative c' {
           \clef percussion
           \time 2/4  
           \repeat volta 2 { g8. g16 d'8 g, }
       }
   &gt;&gt;
" height="38" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/c/r/cre08sn2d1zsf4q0adfpv6hmsfskbne/cre08sn2.png" width="170"/><audio controls="" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Tresillo+_backbeat.mid" id="mwe_player_1" preload="none" style="width:220px;" width="220"><source data-height="0" data-shorttitle="⧼timedmedia-midi⧽ source" data-title="Original ⧼timedmedia-midi⧽ file (0 bps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c6/Tresillo%2B_backbeat.mid" type="audio/midi"/><source data-bandwidth="0" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg Vorbis" data-title="Ogg Vorbis" data-transcodekey="ogg" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/c/c6/Tresillo%2B_backbeat.mid/Tresillo%2B_backbeat.mid.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="0" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/c/c6/Tresillo%2B_backbeat.mid/Tresillo%2B_backbeat.mid.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/></audio></div></dd></dl>
<p>New Orleans native <a href="/wiki/Louis_Moreau_Gottschalk" title="Louis Moreau Gottschalk">Louis Moreau Gottschalk</a>'s piano piece "Ojos Criollos (Danse Cubaine)" (1860) was influenced by the composer's studies in Cuba: the habanera rhythm is clearly heard in the left hand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sublette_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sublette-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 125">: 125 </span></sup> In Gottschalk's symphonic work "A Night in the Tropics" (1859), the tresillo variant <a href="/wiki/Cinquillo" title="Cinquillo">cinquillo</a> appears extensively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SubletteCuba_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-SubletteCuba-66">[66]</a></sup> The figure was later used by Scott Joplin and other ragtime composers.
</p>
<dl><dd><div class="mw-ext-score" data-midi="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/9/4/940h4o3frjny4ctb9lbt9sl1wv1xkpg/940h4o3f.midi"><img alt="
\new RhythmicStaff {
   \clef percussion
   \time 2/4
   \repeat volta 2 { c8 c16 c r[ c c r] }
}
" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/9/4/940h4o3frjny4ctb9lbt9sl1wv1xkpg/940h4o3f.png" width="176"/><audio controls="" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Cinquillo.mid" id="mwe_player_2" preload="none" style="width:220px;" width="220"><source data-height="0" data-shorttitle="⧼timedmedia-midi⧽ source" data-title="Original ⧼timedmedia-midi⧽ file (0 bps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/67/Cinquillo.mid" type="audio/midi"/><source data-bandwidth="0" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg Vorbis" data-title="Ogg Vorbis" data-transcodekey="ogg" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/6/67/Cinquillo.mid/Cinquillo.mid.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="0" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/6/67/Cinquillo.mid/Cinquillo.mid.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/></audio></div></dd></dl>
<p>Comparing the music of New Orleans with the music of Cuba, <a href="/wiki/Wynton_Marsalis" title="Wynton Marsalis">Wynton Marsalis</a> observes that <a href="/wiki/Tresillo_(rhythm)" title="Tresillo (rhythm)">tresillo</a> is the New Orleans "clavé", a Spanish word meaning "code" or "key", as in the key to a puzzle, or mystery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Although the pattern is only half a <a href="/wiki/Clave_(rhythm)" title="Clave (rhythm)">clave</a>, Marsalis makes the point that the single-celled figure is the <a href="/wiki/Bell_pattern" title="Bell pattern">guide-pattern</a> of New Orleans music. <a href="/wiki/Jelly_Roll_Morton" title="Jelly Roll Morton">Jelly Roll Morton</a> called the rhythmic figure the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_tinge" title="Spanish tinge">Spanish tinge</a> and considered it an essential ingredient of jazz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ragtime">Ragtime</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ragtime" title="Ragtime">Ragtime</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scott_Joplin_19072.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="260" data-file-width="200" decoding="async" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Scott_Joplin_19072.jpg/170px-Scott_Joplin_19072.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/ca/Scott_Joplin_19072.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scott_Joplin_19072.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Scott_Joplin" title="Scott Joplin">Scott Joplin</a> in 1903</div></div></div>
<p>The abolition of <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a> in 1865 led to new opportunities for the education of freed African Americans. Although strict segregation limited employment opportunities for most blacks, many were able to find work in entertainment. Black musicians were able to provide entertainment in dances, <a href="/wiki/Minstrel_show" title="Minstrel show">minstrel shows</a>, and in <a href="/wiki/Vaudeville" title="Vaudeville">vaudeville</a>, during which time many marching bands were formed. Black pianists played in bars, clubs, and brothels, as <a href="/wiki/Ragtime" title="Ragtime">ragtime</a> developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke199928,_47_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke199928,_47-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ragtime appeared as sheet music, popularized by African-American musicians such as the entertainer <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Hogan" title="Ernest Hogan">Ernest Hogan</a>, whose hit songs appeared in 1895. Two years later, <a href="/wiki/Vess_Ossman" title="Vess Ossman">Vess Ossman</a> recorded a medley of these songs as a <a href="/wiki/Banjo" title="Banjo">banjo</a> solo known as "Rag Time Medley".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke199928–29_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke199928–29-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Also in 1897, the white composer <a href="/wiki/William_Krell" title="William Krell">William Krell</a> published his "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mississippi_Rag" title="Mississippi Rag">Mississippi Rag</a>" as the first written piano instrumental ragtime piece, and <a href="/wiki/Tom_Turpin" title="Tom Turpin">Tom Turpin</a> published his "Harlem Rag", the first rag published by an African-American.
</p><p>Classically trained pianist <a href="/wiki/Scott_Joplin" title="Scott Joplin">Scott Joplin</a> produced his "<a href="/wiki/Original_Rags" title="Original Rags">Original Rags</a>" in 1898 and, in 1899, had an international hit with "<a href="/wiki/Maple_Leaf_Rag" title="Maple Leaf Rag">Maple Leaf Rag</a>", a multi-<a href="/wiki/Strain_(music)" title="Strain (music)">strain</a> ragtime <a href="/wiki/March_(music)" title="March (music)">march</a> with four parts that feature recurring themes and a bass line with copious <a href="/wiki/Seventh_chord" title="Seventh chord">seventh chords</a>. Its structure was the basis for many other rags, and the <a href="/wiki/Syncopation" title="Syncopation">syncopations</a> in the right hand, especially in the transition between the first and second strain, were novel at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TMG_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-TMG-73">[73]</a></sup> The last four measures of Scott Joplin's "Maple Leaf Rag" (1899) are shown below.
</p>
<dl><dd><div class="mw-ext-score" data-midi="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/d/v/dvtm4x2uf33dvxyjputslc0lvbuaka8/dvtm4x2u.midi"><img alt=" {

             }
            &gt;&gt;

             &lt;es es'&gt; &lt;es' aes c&gt; &lt;es, es'&gt; &lt;e e'&gt;
             &lt;f f'&gt; &lt;f f'&gt; &lt;g g'&gt; &lt;g g'&gt; &lt;aes aes'&gt; &lt;es es'&gt; &lt;aes, aes'&gt; \bar &quot;|.&quot;
             }
         &gt;&gt;
    &gt;&gt;
}
" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/d/v/dvtm4x2uf33dvxyjputslc0lvbuaka8/dvtm4x2u.png" width="602"/><audio controls="" data-durationhint="6" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Maple_Leaf_Rag_seventh_chord_resolution.mid" id="mwe_player_3" preload="none" style="width:220px;" width="220"><source data-bandwidth="771" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="⧼timedmedia-midi⧽ source" data-title="Original ⧼timedmedia-midi⧽ file (771 bps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a8/Maple_Leaf_Rag_seventh_chord_resolution.mid" type="audio/midi"/><source data-bandwidth="120976" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg Vorbis" data-title="Ogg Vorbis" data-transcodekey="ogg" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/a/a8/Maple_Leaf_Rag_seventh_chord_resolution.mid/Maple_Leaf_Rag_seventh_chord_resolution.mid.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="182600" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/a/a8/Maple_Leaf_Rag_seventh_chord_resolution.mid/Maple_Leaf_Rag_seventh_chord_resolution.mid.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/></audio></div></dd></dl>
<p>African-based rhythmic patterns such as <a href="/wiki/Tresillo_(rhythm)" title="Tresillo (rhythm)">tresillo</a> and its variants, the habanera rhythm and <a href="/wiki/Cinquillo" title="Cinquillo">cinquillo</a>, are heard in the ragtime compositions of Joplin and Turpin. Joplin's "<a href="/wiki/Solace_(Joplin)" title="Solace (Joplin)">Solace</a>" (1909) is generally considered to be in the habanera genre:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> both of the pianist's hands play in a syncopated fashion, completely abandoning any sense of a <a href="/wiki/March_(music)" title="March (music)">march</a> rhythm. <a href="/wiki/Ned_Sublette" title="Ned Sublette">Ned Sublette</a> postulates that the tresillo/habanera rhythm "found its way into ragtime and the cakewalk,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> whilst Roberts suggests that "the habanera influence may have been part of what freed black music from ragtime's European bass".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blues">Blues</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Blues" title="Blues">Blues</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="African_genesis">African genesis</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright" style=""><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px"><div class="thumbimage noresize" style="width:300px;">
<div class="mw-ext-score" data-midi="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/o/s/oskrw0rbn1il1pydfxhqprxvrqtfruw/oskrw0rb.midi"><img alt=" {
\override Score.TimeSignature #'stencil = ##f
\relative c' {
  \clef treble \time 6/4
  c4^\markup { &quot;C blues scale&quot; } es f fis g bes c2
} }
" height="55" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/o/s/oskrw0rbn1il1pydfxhqprxvrqtfruw/oskrw0rb.png" width="210"/><div style="margin-top: 3px;"><audio controls=""><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/o/s/oskrw0rbn1il1pydfxhqprxvrqtfruw/oskrw0rb.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/><div>Audio playback is not supported in your browser. <div class="mw-ext-score" data-midi="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/d/7/d74czl67ctqn9k636axwdfnw4v4j5s3/d74czl67.midi"><img alt=" {

  c4^\markup { &quot;C minor pentatonic scale&quot; } es f g bes c2

" height="57" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/d/7/d74czl67ctqn9k636axwdfnw4v4j5s3/d74czl67.png" width="218"/><div style="margin-top: 3px;"><audio controls=""><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/d/7/d74czl67ctqn9k636axwdfnw4v4j5s3/d74czl67.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/><div>Audio playback is not supported in your browser. You can <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/d/7/d74czl67ctqn9k636axwdfnw4v4j5s3/d74czl67.mp3">download the audio file</a>.</div></audio></div></div></div><div class="thumbcaption">A hexatonic <a href="/wiki/Blues_scale" title="Blues scale">blues scale</a> on C, ascending</div></div></div><p>Blues is the name given to both a musical form and a music genre,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> which originated in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African-American" title="African-American">African-American</a> communities of primarily the <a href="/wiki/Deep_South" title="Deep South">Deep South</a> of the United States at the end of the 19th century from their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spiritual_(music)" title="Spiritual (music)">spirituals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Work_song" title="Work song">work songs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Field_holler" title="Field holler">field hollers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ring_shout" title="Ring shout">shouts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chant" title="Chant">chants</a> and rhymed simple narrative <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ballad_(music)" title="Ballad (music)">ballads</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Evolution_of_Differing_Blues_Styles_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Evolution_of_Differing_Blues_Styles-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>The African use of pentatonic scales contributed to the development of <a href="/wiki/Blue_note" title="Blue note">blue notes</a> in blues and jazz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke199911–14_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke199911–14-80">[80]</a></sup> As Kubik explains:
</p>
<blockquote><p>Many of the rural blues of the Deep South are <i>stylistically</i> an extension and merger of basically two broad accompanied song-style traditions in the west central Sudanic belt:
</p><ul><li>A strongly Arabic/Islamic song style, as found for example among the <a href="/wiki/Hausa_people" title="Hausa people">Hausa</a>. It is characterized by melisma, wavy intonation, pitch instabilities within a pentatonic framework, and a declamatory voice.</li>
<li>An ancient west central Sudanic stratum of pentatonic song composition, often associated with simple work rhythms in a regular meter, but with notable off-beat accents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKubik199996_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKubik199996-81">[81]</a></sup></li></ul></blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="W._C._Handy:_early_published_blues">W. C. Handy: early published blues</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WC_Handy_age_19_handyphoto10.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="239" data-file-width="160" decoding="async" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2a/WC_Handy_age_19_handyphoto10.jpg" width="160"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WC_Handy_age_19_handyphoto10.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/W._C._Handy" title="W. C. Handy">W. C. Handy</a> at 19, 1892</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/W._C._Handy" title="W. C. Handy">W. C. Handy</a> became interested in folk blues of the Deep South while traveling through the Mississippi Delta. In this folk blues form, the singer would improvise freely within a limited melodic range, sounding like a field holler, and the guitar accompaniment was slapped rather than strummed, like a small drum which responded in syncopated accents, functioning as another "voice".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Handy and his band members were formally trained African-American musicians who had not grown up with the blues, yet he was able to adapt the blues to a larger band instrument format and arrange them in a popular music form.
</p><p>Handy wrote about his adopting of the blues:
</p>
<blockquote><p>The primitive southern Negro, as he sang, was sure to bear down on the third and seventh tone of the scale, slurring between major and minor. Whether in the cotton field of the Delta or on the Levee up St. Louis way, it was always the same. Till then, however, I had never heard this slur used by a more sophisticated Negro, or by any white man. I tried to convey this effect ... by introducing flat thirds and sevenths (now called blue notes) into my song, although its prevailing key was major ... , and I carried this device into my melody as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Handy,_Father_1941,_p._99_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Handy,_Father_1941,_p._99-83">[83]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The publication of his "<a href="/wiki/The_Memphis_Blues" title="The Memphis Blues">Memphis Blues</a>" sheet music in 1912 introduced the 12-bar blues to the world (although <a href="/wiki/Gunther_Schuller" title="Gunther Schuller">Gunther Schuller</a> argues that it is not really a blues, but "more like a cakewalk").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchuller196866,_145n_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchuller196866,_145n-84">[84]</a></sup> This composition, as well as his later "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Louis_Blues_(song)" title="St. Louis Blues (song)">St. Louis Blues</a>" and others, included the habanera rhythm,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-85">[85]</a></sup> and would become <a href="/wiki/Jazz_standard" title="Jazz standard">jazz standards</a>. Handy's music career began in the pre-jazz era and contributed to the codification of jazz through the publication of some of the first jazz sheet music.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Orleans">New Orleans</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dixieland" title="Dixieland">Dixieland</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bolden_band.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="590" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Bolden_band.gif/220px-Bolden_band.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Bolden_band.gif/330px-Bolden_band.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Bolden_band.gif/440px-Bolden_band.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bolden_band.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Buddy_Bolden" title="Buddy Bolden">The Bolden Band</a> around 1905</div></div></div>
<p>The music of New Orleans had a profound effect on the creation of early jazz. In New Orleans, slaves could practice elements of their culture such as voodoo and playing drums.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Many early jazz musicians played in the bars and brothels of the red-light district around Basin Street called <a href="/wiki/Storyville,_New_Orleans" title="Storyville, New Orleans">Storyville</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke199947,_50_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke199947,_50-87">[87]</a></sup> In addition to dance bands, there were marching bands which played at lavish funerals (later called <a href="/wiki/Jazz_funeral" title="Jazz funeral">jazz funerals</a>). The instruments used by marching bands and dance bands became the instruments of jazz: brass, drums, and reeds tuned in the European 12-tone scale. Small bands contained a combination of self-taught and formally educated musicians, many from the funeral procession tradition. These bands traveled in black communities in the deep south. Beginning in 1914, <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Creole_people" title="Louisiana Creole people">Creole</a> and African-American musicians played in <a href="/wiki/Vaudeville" title="Vaudeville">vaudeville</a> shows which carried jazz to cities in the northern and western parts of the U.S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-creoleorch_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-creoleorch-88">[88]</a></sup> Jazz became international in 1914, when the Creole Band with cornettist <a href="/wiki/Freddie_Keppard" title="Freddie Keppard">Freddie Keppard</a> performed the first ever jazz concert outside the United States, at the <a href="/wiki/Pantages_Playhouse_Theatre" title="Pantages Playhouse Theatre">Pantages Playhouse Theatre</a> in <a href="/wiki/Winnipeg" title="Winnipeg">Winnipeg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>In New Orleans, a white bandleader named <a href="/wiki/Papa_Jack_Laine" title="Papa Jack Laine">Papa Jack Laine</a> integrated blacks and whites in his marching band. He was known as "the father of white jazz" because of the many top players he employed, such as <a href="/wiki/George_Brunies" title="George Brunies">George Brunies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sharkey_Bonano" title="Sharkey Bonano">Sharkey Bonano</a>, and future members of the <a href="/wiki/Original_Dixieland_Jass_Band" title="Original Dixieland Jass Band">Original Dixieland Jass Band</a>. During the early 1900s, jazz was mostly performed in African-American and <a href="/wiki/Mulatto" title="Mulatto">mulatto</a> communities due to segregation laws. Storyville brought jazz to a wider audience through tourists who visited the port city of New Orleans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Many jazz musicians from African-American communities were hired to perform in bars and brothels. These included <a href="/wiki/Buddy_Bolden" title="Buddy Bolden">Buddy Bolden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jelly_Roll_Morton" title="Jelly Roll Morton">Jelly Roll Morton</a> in addition to those from other communities, such as <a href="/wiki/Lorenzo_Tio" title="Lorenzo Tio">Lorenzo Tio</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alcide_Nunez" title="Alcide Nunez">Alcide Nunez</a>. <a href="/wiki/Louis_Armstrong" title="Louis Armstrong">Louis Armstrong</a> started his career in Storyville<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> and found success in Chicago. Storyville was shut down by the U.S. government in 1917.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-web.archive.org_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-web.archive.org-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Syncopation">Syncopation</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:116px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MortonBricktopRowCropMortonFace.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="132" data-file-width="114" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/35/MortonBricktopRowCropMortonFace.jpg" width="114"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MortonBricktopRowCropMortonFace.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jelly_Roll_Morton" title="Jelly Roll Morton">Jelly Roll Morton</a>, in Los Angeles, California, c. 1917 or 1918</div></div></div>
<p>Cornetist Buddy Bolden played in New Orleans from 1895 to 1906. No recordings by him exist. His band is credited with creating the big four: the first syncopated bass drum pattern to deviate from the standard on-the-beat march.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> As the example below shows, the second half of the big four pattern is the habanera rhythm.
</p>
<dl><dd><div class="mw-ext-score" data-midi="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/a/h/ahv6uru6mzxhr0v0qvqgf1ys0viygcq/ahv6uru6.midi"><img alt="
    \new Staff &lt;&lt;
       \relative c' {
           \clef percussion
           \time 4/4  
           \repeat volta 2 { g8 \xNote a' g, \xNote a' g, \xNote a'16. g,32 g8 &lt;g \xNote a'&gt; }
           \repeat volta 2 { r8 \xNote a'\noBeam g, \xNote a' g, \xNote a'16. g,32 g8 &lt;g \xNote a'&gt; }
       }
   &gt;&gt;
" height="57" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/a/h/ahv6uru6mzxhr0v0qvqgf1ys0viygcq/ahv6uru6.png" width="672"/><audio controls="" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Big_four_Buddy_Bolden.mid" id="mwe_player_4" preload="none" style="width:220px;" width="220"><source data-height="0" data-shorttitle="⧼timedmedia-midi⧽ source" data-title="Original ⧼timedmedia-midi⧽ file (0 bps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/71/Big_four_Buddy_Bolden.mid" type="audio/midi"/><source data-bandwidth="0" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg Vorbis" data-title="Ogg Vorbis" data-transcodekey="ogg" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/7/71/Big_four_Buddy_Bolden.mid/Big_four_Buddy_Bolden.mid.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="0" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/7/71/Big_four_Buddy_Bolden.mid/Big_four_Buddy_Bolden.mid.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/></audio></div></dd></dl>
<p>Afro-Creole pianist Jelly Roll Morton began his career in Storyville. Beginning in 1904, he toured with vaudeville shows to southern cities, Chicago, and New York City. In 1905, he composed "<a href="/wiki/Jelly_Roll_Blues" title="Jelly Roll Blues">Jelly Roll Blues</a>", which became the first jazz arrangement in print when it was published in 1915. It introduced more musicians to the New Orleans style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooke199938,_56_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooke199938,_56-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>Morton considered the tresillo/habanera, which he called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_tinge" title="Spanish tinge">Spanish tinge</a>, an essential ingredient of jazz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> "Now in one of my earliest tunes, "New Orleans Blues," you can notice the Spanish tinge. In fact, if you can't manage to put tinges of Spanish in your tunes, you will never be able to get the right seasoning, I call it, for jazz."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>An excerpt of "New Orleans Blues" is shown below. In the excerpt, the left hand plays the tresillo rhythm, while the right hand plays variations on cinquillo.
</p>
<dl><dd><div class="mw-ext-score" data-midi="//upload.wikimedia.org/score/j/e/je93na3r73hbvynmwsyg7pxcpnma0y8/je93na3r.midi"><img alt="
    {
      \new PianoStaff &lt;&lt;
        \new Staff &lt;&lt;
            \relative c'' {
                \clef treble \key bes \major \time 2/2
                f8 &lt;f, f'&gt; &lt;g g'&gt; &lt;f~ cis'&gt; &lt;f d'&gt; &lt;f f'&gt; &lt;g d' g&gt;4
                r8 &lt;f f'&gt; &lt;g g'&gt; &lt;f~ cis'&gt; &lt;f d'&gt; &lt;f f'&gt; &lt;g d' g&gt;4
                r8 &lt;f d' f&gt; &lt;g d' g&gt; &lt;f~ cis'&gt; &lt;f d'&gt; &lt;f d' f&gt; &lt;g d' g&gt; &lt;f d' f&gt;
                }
            &gt;&gt;
